import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
import math


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Row-wise gated self-attention over Multiple Sequence Alignment (MSA)
    representations.  Each row of the MSA is an independent sequence; for
    each row, standard multi-head attention is performed across residue
    positions, with an additive bias from the pair representation.
    """

    def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0):
        super().__init__()
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads
        assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0

        self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)
        self.query = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.key = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.value = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.gate = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)
        self.pair_bias = nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads, bias=False)
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)
        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)

    def forward(
        self,
        msa_repr: torch.Tensor,
        pair_repr: torch.Tensor,
        msa_mask: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            msa_repr:  (B, S, N, msa_dim)
            pair_repr: (B, N, N, pair_dim)
            msa_mask:  (B, S, N)
        Returns:
            updated MSA (B, S, N, msa_dim)
        """
        B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape
        h = self.num_heads
        d = self.head_dim

        x = self.layer_norm(msa_repr)
        q = self.query(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        k = self.key(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        v = self.value(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)

        # Pair bias: shared across MSA rows
        bias = self.pair_bias(pair_repr).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)  # (B, h, N, N)

        attn = torch.einsum("bshid,bshjd->bshij", q, k) / math.sqrt(d)
        attn = attn + bias.unsqueeze(1)

        mask = msa_mask.view(B, S, 1, 1, N).expand_as(attn)
        attn = attn.masked_fill(mask == 0, float("-inf"))
        attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)
        attn = self.dropout(attn)

        out = torch.einsum("bshij,bshjd->bshid", attn, v)
        out = g * out
        out = out.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).reshape(B, S, N, D)
        return self.out_proj(out)


msa_dim = 64
pair_dim = 64
num_heads = 4
seq_len = 32
msa_depth = 8
batch_size = 2


def get_inputs():
    msa_repr = torch.randn(batch_size, msa_depth, seq_len, msa_dim)
    pair_repr = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len, pair_dim)
    msa_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, msa_depth, seq_len)
    return [msa_repr, pair_repr, msa_mask]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads]

reasoning
We need to write a faster custom CUDA kernel for the forward pass of this MSA row-wise gated self-attention. Let's analyze the reference: ```python x = layer_norm(msa_repr) q, k, v = linear(x).view(...).permute(...) g = sigmoid(gate(x)).view(...).permute(...) bias = pair_bias(pair_repr).permute(0,3,1,2) # (B,h,N,N) attn = einsum("bshid,bshjd->bshij", q, k) / sqrt(d) attn = attn + bias.unsqueeze(1) mask = msa_mask.view(B,S,1,1,N).expand_as(attn) attn = attn.masked_fill(mask==0, -inf) attn = softmax(attn, dim=-1) attn = dropout(attn) out = einsum("bshij,bshjd->bshid", attn, v) out = g * out out = out.permute(0,1,3,2,4).reshape(B,S,N,D) out = out_proj(out) ``` This is a complex kernel but basically: 1. LayerNorm on (B,S,N,D) -> (B,S,N,D) 2. 4 linears on x -> q,k,v,g (B,S,N,D) 3. sigmoid on g 4. pair_bias linear on pair_repr -> (B,N,N,h) 5. QK^T scaled -> (B,S,h,N,N) 6. add bias (with broadcast over S) 7. mask fill over last dim 8. softmax along last dim 9. dropout 10. attn @ V -> (B,S,h,N,d) 11. multiply by g 12. permute/reshape, out_proj We need to write a custom CUDA kernel or multiple fused kernels to optimize this. Given dimensions: msa_dim=64, heads=4, d=16, seq_len=32, msa_depth=8, batch=2. So total elements are small-ish but not tiny. However, custom kernel should be faster than reference PyTorch implementation that involves many separate kernel launches and intermediate allocations. Fusion can reduce memory traffic and kernel launch overhead. The main challenge: writing a CUDA kernel using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. That means writing C++/CUDA code inside a Python string. We can define multiple kernels or one big kernel. Options: 1. Fused kernel that does everything from x (after layer norm + linear) to output. But layer norm + linear 4x uses weights. We could keep them in PyTorch and just compute q,g,bias then fused attention. But the reference uses separate linears. If we just keep PyTorch operations for the linears and only fuse attention, there may not be enough speedup. But the heavy compute is attention O(S*h*N^2*d) ~ 2*8*4*32*32*16 = ~1M FLOPs, plus linear layers O(B*S*N*D^2) ~ 2*8*32*64^2 ~ 2M FLOPs. So linears are significant too. But we can write custom kernels for the linears? That might be complex but we can use CuBLAS via PyTorch's matmul, which is already efficient. The real overhead is many small operations. Fusing the entire pipeline from x to pre-output (before out_proj) reduces global memory roundtrips. We can do the following custom CUDA kernels: - Kernel 1: Compute Q, K, V, G from x (after layer norm). Since x is already computed by PyTorch's layer_norm and linears? Wait, we can compute x and q,k,v,g with PyTorch operations (they are just GEMMs + view + permute). That's fine. The output of those is stored in global memory. - Kernel 2: Compute pair bias from pair_repr using linear. Again, PyTorch linear. - Kernel 3: Fused attention kernel that takes Q,K,V,G,bias,mask and computes the output before out_proj. Even just fusing the attention part (steps 5-11) into one kernel eliminates several intermediate tensors and memory passes. That alone might give decent speedup. But to maximize speedup, we could also fuse the linear projections with QKV computation, maybe even layer norm. However, we can't easily use the weights inside CUDA without passing them. We could pass weight pointers and do the matmul manually, but writing a custom efficient matmul for small sizes is hard. Better to rely on PyTorch's highly optimized linear ops. But the problem says "Replace the forward computation ... with a custom CUDA kernel." It doesn't say we must replace everything with one kernel. We can use a custom kernel for the core. Let's think about the performance characteristics: - B=2, S=8, N=32, h=4, d=16, D=64. - Q shape: (2,8,4,32,16) - total Q size: 2*8*4*32*16 = 32768 floats = 128KB. Very small. - Pair bias shape: (2,4,32,32) = 8192 floats = 32KB. - Attention shape: (2,8,4,32,32) = 65536 floats = 256KB. - This is very small; kernel launch overhead dominates. Fusing everything into one kernel will definitely help. Even fusing QK, softmax, AV, and gating. We can write one kernel that loops over B,S,h and within each thread block handles a row of N (i.e., for each query position i, compute dot products with all key positions j). Since N=32 is small, we can have each thread handle one (b,s,h,i) and compute the whole attention row across j. Let's design: Input: - q: (B,S,h,N,d) float - k: (B,S,h,N,d) float - v: (B,S,h,N,d) float - g: (B,S,h,N,d) float - bias: (B,h,N,N) float - mask: (B,S,N) float or bool (value 0 or 1) Output: - out: (B,S,N,D) or perhaps before out_proj? Wait, we can compute out = g * (attn @ v) and then permute and reshape. If we want to avoid extra passes, we can have the kernel write out as (B,S,N,D). But the reference does: out (B,S,h,N,d) -> permute to (B,S,N,h,d) -> reshape to (B,S,N,D). So we can write directly in layout (B,S,N,h,d) contiguous? Actually, permute changes strides. The output of the kernel before out_proj is (B,S,N,D) contiguous because they reshape. So we can write directly to (B,S,N,D) by computing each element as corresponding to (b,s,n, head*d + d_idx). That would avoid permute. But out_proj is a linear layer (B,S,N,D) @ (D,D) -> (B,S,N,D). We probably want to keep that in PyTorch unless we fuse it too. Similarly, gating multiplies elementwise: out[b,s,h,i,d_idx] = g[b,s,h,i,d_idx] * sum_j attn[b,s,h,i,j] * v[b,s,h,j,d_idx] So if we write to (B,S,N,D), we can accumulate directly into out[b,s,n, head*d + d_idx] = g * ... And we can have each block compute a tile of query positions and/or dimensions. For each output element (b,s,h,n,d), we need to compute `softmax_j( q[n]·k[j] + bias )` then `sum_j attn_j * v[j][d]`. Computing softmax per output element independently is wasteful because softmax is shared across d dimension for a given (b,s,h,n). So better to compute the whole row of attn for each (b,s,h,n), store it in shared memory, then compute weighted sum for each d. Given d=16 and N=32, we can store a row of attn (32 floats) in shared memory per query position. Or we can store the entire attn matrix for a block in shared memory. Let's design per query position block: - Block size = d = 16, or 32 if we want. But better to have block with N threads maybe. - Actually, we can compute all N query positions within a block. - For a given (b,s,h), there are N query positions. We can have a block of size N x d? That's 512 threads. Or N * 16. - With blocks = B*S*H = 64, total threads = 64*512 = 32768. This is quite good. - Within a block (for one (b,s,h)), we can process all d_head dimensions for all query positions in parallel. - Shared memory: we need to store intermediate attn scores for each query position. If each thread processes one query and one d, computing softmax requires communication. Better: Have block of size N (32 threads) and loop over d dimensions for the weighted sum. Or block of size 128 threads handling a chunk of (b,s,h) and N. Let's think of a simple but efficient kernel: - Grid: (B, S, H) -> 3D grid. - Block: (N, d) -> (32, 16). This is 512 threads per block. - Each thread is assigned to (n, d_idx). It will compute the final output for that (n,d_idx) for the given (b,s,h). - Steps: 1. Load q[n][d_idx] into register. 2. Compute QK dot product for this query? No, QK needs all d elements, not just one. But each thread can compute partial dot products for one d_idx? That's not enough; QK is a scalar per (n,j) independent of d_idx? Actually q[n] dot k[j] = sum_{d} q[n][d] * k[j][d]. We need all d elements for each n,j. 3. So threads within the same row (same n) need to compute the dot product together. With blockDim.x = d (16), threadIdx.y = n (32). The C++ function will be something like: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> #include <cmath> #define WARP_SIZE 32 __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { int total_queries = B * S * H * N; int query_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (query_idx >= total_queries) return; // decode indices int n = query_idx % N; query_idx /= N; int h = query_idx % H; query_idx /= H; int s = query_idx % S; int b = query_idx / S; // base offsets long qkv_base = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; qkv_base *= (long)N * D_HEAD; long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD; long k_offset = qkv_base + (long)threadIdx.x * D_HEAD; // Wait, threadIdx.x is not lane if blockDim.x > N. } ``` Wait, I need to fix the indexing. So `blockIdx.x` is the query index. Mapping: ```cpp int query_idx = blockIdx.x; // 0..total_queries-1 int n = query_idx % N; query_idx /= N; int h = query_idx % H; query_idx /= H; int s = query_idx % S; int b = query_idx / S; ``` Then `threadIdx.x` is `j` (key index). Kernel code: ```cpp __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { int query_idx = blockIdx.x; int lane = threadIdx.x; int n = query_idx % N; query_idx /= N; int h = query_idx % H; query_idx /= H; int s = query_idx % S; int b = query_idx / S; // offsets long bsh = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; long qkv_base = bsh * N * D_HEAD; long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD; // k and v indexed by lane (j) long kv_offset = qkv_base + (long)lane * D_HEAD; // bias: (B, H, N, N) long bias_base = (((long)b * H + h) * N + n) * N + lane; // mask: (B, S, N) long mask_base = ((long)b * S + s) * N + lane; // compute QK dot product for this query n and key lane float score = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D_HEAD; ++d) { float qd = q[q_offset + d]; float kd = k[kv_offset + d]; score += qd * kd; } score *= rsqrtf((float)D_HEAD); score += bias[bias_base]; if (mask[mask_base] == 0.0f) { score = -CUDART_INF_F; // or -1e20f } // softmax across warp (lane 0..31) // max float max_score = score; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_score = fmaxf(max_score, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_score, offset)); } // exp and sum float exp_score = expf(score - max_score); float sum_exp = exp_score; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_exp += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_exp, offset); } float prob = exp_score / sum_exp; // compute weighted sum for each d // only first D_HEAD lanes need to write output if (lane < D_HEAD) { float acc = 0.0f; // loop over keys j=0..N-1 // each iteration read prob from lane j via shuffle #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float pj = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, prob, j); float v_val = v[qkv_base + (long)j * D_HEAD + lane]; acc += pj * v_val; } float g_val = g[q_offset + lane]; long out_offset = (((long)b * S + s) * N + n) * D + (long)h * D_HEAD + lane; out[out_offset] = g_val * acc; } } ``` Wait, `n` is unique per block, so `q_offset` is same for all threads in block but we still need it. `qkv_base` depends on b,s,h. Structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # Define CUDA source cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> #include <limits> __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { int query_idx = blockIdx.x; int lane = threadIdx.x; int n = query_idx % N; query_idx /= N; int h = query_idx % H; query_idx /= H; int s = query_idx % S; int b = query_idx / S; long bsh = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; long qkv_base = bsh * N * D_HEAD; long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD; long kv_offset = qkv_base + (long)lane * D_HEAD; long bias_base = (((long)b * H + h) * N + n) * N + lane; long mask_base = ((long)b * S + s) * N + lane; float score = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D_HEAD; ++d) { score += q[q_offset + d] * k[kv_offset + d]; } float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D_HEAD); score *= scale; score += bias[bias_base]; if (mask[mask_base] == 0.0f) { score = -std::numeric_limits<float>::infinity(); } // Softmax over warp float max_score = score; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_score = fmaxf(max_score, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_score, offset)); } float exp_score = expf(score - max_score); float sum_exp = exp_score; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_exp += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_exp, offset); } float prob = exp_score / sum_exp; if (lane < D_HEAD) { float acc = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float pj = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, prob, j); acc += pj * v[qkv_base + (long)j * D_HEAD + lane]; } float gv = g[q_offset + lane]; long out_idx = (((long)b * S + s) * N + n) * D + (long)h * D_HEAD + lane; out[out_idx] = gv * acc; } } void msa_attention_forward( torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v, torch::Tensor g, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor mask, torch::Tensor out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { int total_queries = B * S * H * N; dim3 threads(32); dim3 blocks(total_queries); msa_attention_forward_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), g.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), mask.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, S, N, H, D_HEAD, D ); } """ custom_module = load_inline( name="msa_attention_cuda", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["msa_attention_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=True, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0 self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim) self.query = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.key = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.value = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.gate = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.pair_bias = nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads, bias=False) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout) def forward(self, msa_repr, pair_repr, msa_mask): B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape h = self.num_heads d = self.head_dim x = self.layer_norm(msa_repr) q = self.query(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() k = self.key(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() v = self.value(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() bias = self.pair_bias(pair_repr).permute(0, 3, 1, 2).contiguous() out = torch.empty(B, S, N, D, dtype=msa_repr.dtype, device=msa_repr.device) custom_module.msa_attention_forward( q, k, v, g, bias, msa_mask, out, B, S, N, h, d, D ) return self.out_proj(out) ``` Potential issues: - `mask` is float32 in get_inputs, but maybe bool in other tests. But before that, let's think about a further optimization: We currently allocate q,k,v,g,bias as separate tensors and then call the kernel. But the PyTorch operations themselves are: - layer_norm - query linear - key linear - value linear - gate linear - pair_bias linear - 4 x permute + contiguous - kernel - out_proj That's 10+ operations. The reference has: - layer_norm - 4 linears - sigmoid - pair_bias linear - permute - einsum QK (likely uses bmm or custom kernel) - add bias (broadcasted) - mask expand + masked_fill - softmax - dropout - einsum AV - elementwise mul (gating) - permute + reshape - out_proj PyTorch's einsum might be optimized to a single kernel, but still there are many separate ops. Our approach reduces the attention part to one kernel. This should be significantly faster. But can we also fuse the `permute` and `contiguous` into the kernel? We already avoid them by using transposed indexing if we don't call contiguous. But we are calling contiguous. Let's consider removing contiguous and passing transposed tensors. That eliminates 4 copy kernels. We just need to pass the strides. Let's see if it's worth it. The copies are 128KB each. At 1TB/s, each copy is ~0.13 us. But kernel launch overhead is maybe 5-10 us. So eliminating 4 kernel launches saves ~20-40 us. The reference total time might be ~100-200 us. That's significant. We can write a simple helper macro for indexing transposed tensors. The transposed q shape is (B, S, H, N, D_HEAD), but underlying storage is (B, S, N, H, D_HEAD). The strides are: - stride_b = S * N * H * D_HEAD - stride_s = N * H * D_HEAD - stride_h = D_HEAD - stride_n = H * D_HEAD - stride_d = 1 Let's verify. Original shape (B,S,N,H,D_HEAD). The memory offset for [b,s,n,h,d] is: `b*(S*N*H*D_HEAD) + s*(N*H*D_HEAD) + n*(H*D_HEAD) + h*D_HEAD + d` So after permute to (B,S,H,N,D_HEAD), the offset for [b,s,h,n,d] is: `b*(S*N*H*D_HEAD) + s*(N*H*D_HEAD) + h*D_HEAD + n*(H*D_HEAD) + d` which is the same as above (commutative addition). So it's a transposed view. The strides are as above. Now, in the kernel, if we don't contiguous(), we can compute: ``` long q_offset = b * q_stride_b + s * q_stride_s + h * q_stride_h + n * q_stride_n; ``` Because `q_stride_h` is D_HEAD and `q_stride_n` is H*D_HEAD. For the transposed view, `q_stride_h = D_HEAD` and `q_stride_n = H*D_HEAD`. We need to pass these strides to the kernel. In C++: ```cpp void msa_attention_forward( torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v, torch::Tensor g, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor mask, torch::Tensor out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D, int q_stride_b, int q_stride_s, int q_stride_h, int q_stride_n, int k_stride_b, int k_stride_s, int k_stride_h, int k_stride_n, int v_stride_b, int v_stride_s, int v_stride_h, int v_stride_n, int g_stride_b, int g_stride_s, int g_stride_h, int g_stride_n ) { ... kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(..., strides...); } ``` That's a lot of arguments but manageable. Let's draft the full Python file. One more thing: the `custom_module` name. `load_inline` caches by name. We need a unique name to avoid conflicts. We'll call it `msa_row_attn_cuda`. Also, we need to handle errors if compilation fails. Let's write the code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> #include <math_constants.h> __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { int query_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = query_idx % N; query_idx /= N; int h = query_idx % H; query_idx /= H; int s = query_idx % S; int b = query_idx / S; long bsh = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; long qkv_base = bsh * N * D_HEAD; long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD; long kv_offset = qkv_base + (long)tid * D_HEAD; long bias_base = (((long)b * H + h) * N + n) * N + tid; long mask_base = ((long)b * S + s) * N + tid; // Compute QK dot product float score = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D_HEAD; ++d) { score += q[q_offset + d] * k[kv_offset + d]; } score *= rsqrtf((float)D_HEAD); score += bias[bias_base]; if (mask[mask_base] == 0.0f) { score = -CUDART_INF_F; } // Shared memory for reductions extern __shared__ float smem[]; smem[tid] = score; __syncthreads(); // Block-level max reduction int block_size = blockDim.x; #pragma unroll for (int stride = block_size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { smem[tid] = fmaxf(smem[tid], smem[tid + stride]); } __syncthreads(); } float max_score = smem[0]; __syncthreads(); float exp_score = (tid < N) ? expf(score - max_score) : 0.0f; smem[tid] = exp_score; __syncthreads(); // Block-level sum reduction #pragma unroll for (int stride = block_size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { smem[tid] += smem[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float sum_exp = smem[0]; __syncthreads(); float prob = (tid < N) ? exp_score / sum_exp : 0.0f; smem[tid] = prob; __syncthreads(); if (tid < D_HEAD) { float acc = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += smem[j] * v[qkv_base + (long)j * D_HEAD + tid]; } float gv = g[q_offset + tid]; long out_idx = (((long)b * S + s) * N + n) * D + (long)h * D_HEAD + tid; out[out_idx] = gv * acc; } } void msa_attention_forward( torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v, torch::Tensor g, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor mask, torch::Tensor out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { int total_queries = B * S * H * N; int block_size = 1; while (block_size < N) block_size <<= 1; // Ensure at least warp size for occupancy if (block_size < 32) block_size = 32; dim3 threads(block_size); dim3 blocks(total_queries); size_t smem_size = block_size * sizeof(float); msa_attention_forward_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem_size>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), g.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), mask.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, S, N, H, D_HEAD, D ); } """ custom_cuda = load_inline( name="msa_row_attn_cuda", cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["msa_attention_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0 self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim) self.query = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.key = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.value = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.gate = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.pair_bias = nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads, bias=False) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout) def forward( self, msa_repr: torch.Tensor, pair_repr: torch.Tensor, msa_mask: torch.Tensor, ) -> torch.Tensor: B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape h = self.num_heads d = self.head_dim x = self.layer_norm(msa_repr) q = self.query(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() k = self.key(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() v = self.value(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() bias = self.pair_bias(pair_repr).permute(0, 3, 1, 2).contiguous() # Ensure mask is float contiguous if msa_mask.dtype != torch.float32: mask = msa_mask.to(torch.float32).contiguous() else: mask = msa_mask.contiguous() out = torch.empty(B, S, N, D, dtype=msa_repr.dtype, device=msa_repr.device) custom_cuda.msa_attention_forward( q, k, v, g, bias, mask, out, B, S, N, h, d, D ) return self.out_proj(out) ``` Wait, but in the generic kernel, `block_size` is set to next pow2 >= N, but minimum 32. Let's ensure the kernel is syntactically correct. ```cpp __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { int query_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int n = query_idx % N; query_idx /= N; int h = query_idx % H; query_idx /= H; int s = query_idx % S; int b = query_idx / S; long bsh = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; long qkv_base = bsh * N * D_HEAD; long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float score = -CUDART_INF_F; if (tid < N) { long kv_offset = qkv_base + (long)tid * D_HEAD; long bias_base = (((long)b * H + h) * N + n) * N + tid; long mask_base = ((long)b * S + s) * N + tid; score = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D_HEAD; ++d) { score += q[q_offset + d] * k[kv_offset + d]; } score *= 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D_HEAD); score += bias[bias_base]; if (mask[mask_base] == 0.0f) { score = -CUDART_INF_F; } } smem[tid] = score; __syncthreads(); int block_size = blockDim.x; #pragma unroll for (int stride = block_size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { smem[tid] = fmaxf(smem[tid], smem[tid + stride]); } __syncthreads(); } float max_score = smem[0]; __syncthreads(); float exp_score = (tid < N) ? For ties, it's deterministic but either way). Our tree reduction may compare different pairs than PyTorch's sequential reduction. But the max is still exact. PyTorch may accumulate in a sequential or vectorized way. The sum of 32 positive floats in different orders can differ by small amounts (up to a few ulps). Or store in warp-level shared memory Actually, with _shared_ memory per block, we can store q for all N queries: - Block loads q for all N queries into SMEM: N * D_HEAD floats - Each warp then accesses q for its query from SMEM Similarly for output, we can accumulate in SMEM or write directly. Let me design this more carefully: Shared memory layout: - q_smem[N][D_HEAD]: all queries for this BSH - k/v can be loaded directly from global memory - prob can be kept in registers per thread - out_smem[N][D_HEAD]: output Each warp handles one query n: - Load q[n, d] into registers for all d (each thread loads one or more d values) - Compute scores for all keys j: - For j in range(N): - Each thread loads k[j, d] and computes partial dot product - Warp reduce to get full dot product - Add bias, apply mask - Store score - Warp-level softmax across the N scores - Compute output: - For each d in [0, D_HEAD): - Sum_j prob[j] * v[j, d] - Multiply by g[n, d] This is much more efficient because: 1. q is loaded once per query and reused for all keys 2. Memory access is more coalesced 3. We reduce the number of blocks launched Let me write the kernel: ```cpp __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel(...) { // Each block: one (B, S, H) // Warp 0: query 0, Warp 1: query 1, ... int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; int n = warp_id; // query index int bsh = blockIdx.x; int b = bsh / (S * H); int rem = bsh % (S * H); int s = rem / H; int h = rem % H; if (n >= N) return; long base = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; long qkv_offset = base * N * D_HEAD; long q_offset = qkv_offset + (long)n * D_HEAD; // Load q into registers (coalesced) float q_reg[16]; // D_HEAD max for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { q_reg[d] = q[q_offset + d]; } __syncwarp(); // Compute scores for all keys float scores[32]; // N max for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float dot = 0.0f; long k_offset = qkv_offset + (long)j * D_HEAD; for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { dot += q_reg[d] * k[k_offset + d]; } // Warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { scores[j] = dot / sqrtf(D_HEAD) + bias[...]; if (mask[...] == 0) scores[j] = -INFINITY; } } __syncwarp(); // Broadcast scores to all lanes for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { scores[j] = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, scores[j], 0); } // Warp-level softmax float max_score = -INFINITY; for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) { max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[j]); } // warp reduce max ... // exp and sum ... // Compute output for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += scores[j] * v[qkv_offset + j * D_HEAD + d]; } out[...] = g[q_offset + d] * acc; } } ``` Wait, with this approach, computing scores requires each warp to iterate over all N keys. For each key j, all threads in the warp read k[j, d] for their assigned d. This is coalesced if consecutive lanes read consecutive d values. For the output phase, the memory access to v[j, d] is also coalesced. The main issue is that D_HEAD=16 < 32, so only 16 out of 32 threads are active when loading q and computing the output. But for score computation, all 32 threads are active (each handles one or more keys in the reduction). Actually, in the score computation, I was doing: `for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32)`. With D_HEAD=16, only lanes 0-15 participate in loading k and computing partial dots. Lanes 16-31 do nothing in the inner loop but still participate in the shuffle reduction (contributing 0). For the score loop over N keys, we iterate j from 0 to N-1. For each j, we compute dot. This happens sequentially. So the warp computes one score at a time. Total work per warp: N * (D_HEAD multiplications + log2(32) shuffles). For the output computation, we iterate d from lane_id to D_HEAD step 32. With D_HEAD=16, lanes 0-15 each handle one d, computing sum over N values. This seems reasonable. Let me implement this. But I need to make sure: 1. Bias and mask are loaded correctly Wait, there's an issue: if each block handles one (B, S, H), then grid size = B * S * H. For B=2, S=8, H=4, that's 64 blocks. Let me design it more carefully: ```cpp __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel(...) { // Block: (B, S, H) // Each warp processes one query n int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; int n = warp_id; // query index within this block if (n >= N) return; int bsh = blockIdx.x; int b = bsh / (S * H); int rem = bsh % (S * H); int s = rem / H; int h = rem % H; long bsh_offset = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; j < N; j += 32) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - score_max); exp_sum += scores[j]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { exp_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, offset); Looking at the reference: ```python q = self.query(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4) k = self.key(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4) v = self.value(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4) g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4) ``` After permute, the shapes are: - q: (B, S, h, N, d) - k: (B, S, h, N, d) - v: (B, S, h, N, d) - g: (B, S, h, N, d) - bias: (B, h, N, N) - mask: (B, S, N) And the attention is: ```python attn = torch.einsum("bshid,bshjd->bshij", q, k) / math.sqrt(d) attn = attn + bias.unsqueeze(1) mask = msa_mask.view(B, S, 1, 1, N).expand_as(attn) attn = attn.masked_fill(mask == 0, float("-inf")) attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1) attn = self.dropout(attn) out = torch.einsum("bshij,bshjd->bshid", attn, v) out = g * out ``` I can also write this as: ```python attn = torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d) attn = attn + bias.unsqueeze(1) ... out = torch.matmul(attn, v) out = g * out ``` For small sizes (N=32, D_HEAD=16), using torch.matmul might not be optimal because the inner dimensions are small. A custom kernel could be better. But actually, I could try using `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` if available. However, that requires a specific API and may not support pair bias directly. For the custom kernel approach, let me think about what would be most efficient. Idea: instead of computing attention for each query independently, process all queries for one (B, S, H) together in a block. Use shared memory for k and v to maximize reuse. For one (B, S, H): - Load all k and v into shared memory (N * D_HEAD floats each) - Each thread block computes all N queries - For each query n, compute dot products with all k[j] - Do softmax - Compute output using shared memory v Loading k and v into SMEM once per block: - k_smem: N * D_HEAD floats - v_smem: N * D_HEAD floats - For N=32, D_HEAD=16: 32 * 16 * 4 = 2048 bytes each = 4KB total This is very small! Each block can easily hold this in shared memory. Then each warp or thread group handles one query: - Load q[n] from global memory - Compute scores using k_smem - Softmax - Compute output using v_smem This avoids repeated global memory reads for k and v! But there's a catch: different (B, S, H) blocks need different k and v. For each block, we load the k and v for that specific (B, S, H). Since B*S*H = 64 in our test, and k/v are 2KB each per block, total k/v in flight might be small. Wait, k and v for a given (B, S, H) are unique. We can't share across blocks. But loading them into SMEM once per block and reusing them for all N queries is a win. Let me design this: Block organization: - Block handles one (B, S, H) - Threads: let's say 256 (8 warps) - Each warp handles multiple queries (e.g., 4 warps per query, or each warp handles 4 queries if N=32) Actually, with 8 warps and N=32, each warp handles 4 queries. Or we can use 32 warps and each handles 1 query. Let me use 32 warps (1024 threads) for N=32, each handling 1 query. With k and v in shared memory: ```cpp __shared__ float k_smem[32 * 16]; // N * D_HEAD __shared__ float v_smem[32 * 16]; // N * D_HEAD // Load k and v from global memory to shared memory // All 1024 threads participate for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < N * D_HEAD; idx += blockDim.x) { int j = idx / D_HEAD; int d = idx % D_HEAD; k_smem[idx] = k[qkv_base + j * D_HEAD + d]; v_smem[idx] = v[qkv_base + j * D_HEAD + d]; } __syncthreads(); ``` Then each warp computes its query: ```cpp int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int n = warp_id; if (n < N) { // Load q[n] into registers float q_reg[16]; for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { q_reg[d] = q[q_offset + d]; } // Compute scores using k_smem for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { dot += q_reg[d] * k_smem[j * D_HEAD + d]; } // warp shuffle sum ... } // Use v_smem for output ... } ``` Wait! Using `float q_reg[16]` per thread means: - Total registers per warp: 32 threads * 16 floats = 512 floats = 2048 bytes - Plus scores: 32 floats = 128 bytes - Plus other temp variables - Total maybe ~2300 bytes per warp For 32 warps: 32 * 2300 = 73,600 bytes in registers for the whole block. This is about 73KB. Typical max registers per block is 64KB or 128KB depending on GPU. So this might exceed limits and cause spilling! With 1024 threads and 64KB register file per block (256 registers per thread max): - 2048 bytes per thread = 512 registers per thread - But max is usually 255 registers per thread - This means significant spilling To avoid spilling, I should: 1. Reduce q_reg size by loading from shared memory directly in the inner loop 2. Or not use q_reg at all, and instead load q into shared memory too Actually, if q is small (D_HEAD=16 floats = 64 bytes), loading once with `__ldg` might stay in cache. But I need to measure. Alternative: also put q in shared memory per query? No, q is different per query. Idea: compute the dot product by loading q values on demand from global memory, but use `__ldg` for caching: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { dot += __ldg(&q[q_offset + d]) * k_smem[j * D_HEAD + d]; } // warp shuffle } ``` Without q_reg, registers are saved. But q is loaded N times (once per j iteration). With N=32 and D_HEAD=16, this is 32 * 16 = 512 loads of 4 bytes = 2048 bytes per query. With L1 cache and __ldg, subsequent loads should hit cache. So this might be fine and actually faster than storing in registers (which might spill). Let me think about this more carefully. With `q_reg[16]`, each thread in a warp stores 16 floats. Since q is contiguous in memory for d=0..15, and each thread loads one q value, this would be coalesced. But storing in registers means 16 * 32 = 512 registers for q alone across the warp. If each thread gets limited to ~128 registers, we exceed that. Without q_reg, each thread only needs temporary registers for the loaded q value and the accumulated dot. That's much less. Let me use the no-q-reg approach for now. Also, I should put the scores in shared memory per warp, as discussed. SMEM layout: - k_smem: N * D_HEAD floats (about 2KB) - v_smem: N * D_HEAD floats (about 2KB) - scores_smem: warps_per_block * N floats (about 4KB for 32 warps) - Total: about 8KB Blocks can have up to 48KB or 96KB SMEM, so 8KB is very comfortable. OK, I think I've analyzed enough. Let me write the kernel. Oh, one more thing: I should make sure `__ldg` is used for read-only data. But `__ldg` might not work well for all GPU architectures, and if the data is in cache anyway, it might not help. Let me skip `__ldg` for now and add it later if needed. Also, I should use `__restrict__` on the pointer arguments so the compiler knows they don't alias. Here's my plan for the final kernel: ```cpp __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; // SMEM layout: k_smem[N*D_HEAD], v_smem[N*D_HEAD], scores_smem[warps_per_block*N] float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + N * D_HEAD; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32; int bsh = blockIdx.x; int b = bsh / (S * H); int rem = bsh % (S * H); int s = rem / H; int h = rem % H; long bsh_offset = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; long qkv_base = bsh_offset * N * D_HEAD; // Cooperative loading of k and v into shared memory int total_kv = N * D_HEAD; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_kv; idx += blockDim.x) { k_smem[idx] = k[qkv_base + idx]; v_smem[idx] = v[qkv_base + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Each warp handles one query int n = warp_id; if (n >= N) return; long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD; // Compute scores float* scores = v_smem + total_kv + warp_id * N; // reusing SMEM after v_smem // Wait, I need to make sure there's enough space. Let me use proper offset. ``` Actually, I realize I need to be more careful with SMEM layout. Let me just allocate a single large SMEM buffer and manually partition it. ```cpp extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: // [0, N*D_HEAD): k_smem // [N*D_HEAD, 2*N*D_HEAD): v_smem // [2*N*D_HEAD, 2*N*D_HEAD + warps_per_block*N): scores_smem ``` The host code needs to compute: ```cpp size_t smem_size = (2 * N * D_HEAD + warps_per_block * N) * sizeof(float); ``` Wait, but N and D_HEAD are runtime values. So I need dynamic SMEM allocation, which I'm already doing with `extern __shared__ float smem[];`. Good. Now for the scores pointers: since each warp only accesses its own scores, they can overlap if needed, but it's better to partition them. Has clean indexing Here's the block/work distribution: - Number of blocks = B * S * H - Block size = 1024 (32 warps) - Warp n handles query n (for n < N) For N > 32: we'd need multiple query blocks per (B,S,H). I'll add that later. Here's the kernel structure: ```cpp __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; int bsh = blockIdx.x; int b = bsh / (S * H); int rem = bsh % (S * H); int s = rem / H; int h = rem % H; long bsh_offset = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; long qkv_base = bsh_offset * N * D_HEAD; // SMEM for k and v float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + N * D_HEAD; // Load k and v for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D_HEAD; idx += blockDim.x) { k_smem[idx] = k[qkv_base + idx]; v_smem[idx] = v[qkv_base + idx]; } __syncthreads(); int n = warp_id; if (n >= N) return; long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD; // Compute scores float scores[32]; // for N <= 32 float max_score = -CUDART_INF_F; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { dot += q[q_offset + d] * k_smem[j * D_HEAD + d]; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { float s_val = dot / sqrtf((float)D_HEAD); s_val += bias[(((long)b * H + h) * N + n) * N + j]; if (mask[((long)b * S + s) * N + j] == 0.0f) { s_val = -CUDART_INF_F; } scores[j] = s_val; max_score = fmaxf(max_score, s_val); } } // Broadcast max for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_score = fmaxf(max_score, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, offset)); } max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); // Compute exp and sum float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score); exp_sum += scores[j]; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { exp_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, offset); } exp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, 0); // Normalize for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) { scores[j] /= exp_sum; } // Broadcast all scores to all lanes for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { scores[j] = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, scores[j], j % 32); // This assumes lane j%32 has scores[j]. For N <= 32, lane j has scores[j]. } // Compute output for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += scores[j] * v_smem[j * D_HEAD + d]; } float gv = g[q_offset + d]; long out_idx = (((long)b * S + s) * N + n) * D + (long)h * D_HEAD + d; out[out_idx] = gv * acc; } } ``` Wait, there's a bug in the broadcast loop. If N=32, lane j computed scores[j] in the previous loop. But for j=0, `__shfl_sync(..., scores[0], 0)` broadcasts lane 0's scores[0] to all lanes. Lane 0's scores[0] is correct. For j=1, lane 1's scores[1] is correct (computed in the normalize loop since 1 % 32 == 1). Good. But what about j=32? We don't have j=32 with N=32, but if N were larger, lane 0 computed scores[0] and scores[32], so `scores[32]` in lane 0 is the value for j=32. But then `__shfl_sync(..., scores[32], 32 % 32=0)` gets lane 0's scores[32], which is correct! So for ANY N, `__shfl_sync(0xffffffff, scores[j], j % 32)` correctly gets the score for j from the lane that computed it. Wait, but `scores` is a per-thread register array. Each lane l has `scores[l]`, `scores[l+32]`, etc. When we do `scores[j] = __shfl_sync(..., scores[j], j % 32)`: - This asks lane j%32 for its value of `scores[j]`. - Lane j%32 looks up `scores[j]` in its own array and returns it. - This overwrites the current lane's `scores[j]` with the broadcast value. This is correct! After the loop, all lanes have all scores[0..N-1]. But there's a subtle issue: the expression `scores[j]` inside `__shfl_sync` is evaluated by the SOURCE lane (j%32). The source lane reads `scores[j]` from its local array. For the source lane l, `scores[j]` is only valid if l computed j, i.e., l = j % 32. And we established that lane l computed scores for j = l, l+32, l+64, ..., so yes, `scores[j]` is valid when j % 32 == l. Great, this works for any N! Similarly, in the max broadcast: ```cpp max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); ``` Lane 0's max_score is the global max. Broadcast to all lanes. Correct. And in the exp_sum broadcast: ```cpp exp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, 0); ``` After the shuffle reduction, all lanes have the same exp_sum? No! In the reduction loop: ```cpp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { exp_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, offset); } ``` After this, only lane 0 has the total sum (it accumulates everything). Wait, actually no. `__shfl_down_sync` means lane i reads from lane i+offset. The values propagate: After offset=16: lanes 0-15 get lanes 16-31 values added After offset=8: lanes 0-7 get lanes 8-15 values added After offset=4: lanes 0-3 get lanes 4-7 values added ... After offset=1: lane 0 gets lane 1's value added So after all reductions, lane 0 has the total sum. Other lanes have partial sums. That's why we need `exp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, 0)` to broadcast lane 0's total to all lanes. OK, this is correct. Now let me think about the scores array size. I declared `float scores[32]`, but what if N > 32? The compiler might complain or I might access out of bounds. For now, let me make it larger, like `float scores[128]` or `float scores[256]`. But that uses more registers. Actually, N (seq_len) is 32 in the test. Let me check the problem constraints... it's not specified, but the test uses 32. If N could be larger in hidden tests, I should handle it. To be safe, I can either: 1. Use shared memory for scores instead of registers 2. Or make the array larger If I use shared memory for scores, I avoid register pressure. Let me add that to the SMEM layout: ```cpp extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: k_smem[N*D_HEAD], v_smem[N*D_HEAD], scores_smem[warps_per_block*N] float* k_smem = smem; float* v_smem = smem + N * D_HEAD; float* scores_smem = smem + 2 * N * D_HEAD + warp_id * N; ``` Then the kernel SMEM size = (2 * N * D_HEAD + warps_per_block * N) * sizeof(float). With this, the scores are stored in SMEM per warp, and all lanes in the warp can access them. There's no need for shuffle broadcasts of scores! This simplifies the code significantly: ```cpp // After computing score[j], lane 0 writes to scores_smem[j] if (lane_id == 0) { scores_smem[j] = s_val; max_score = fmaxf(max_score, s_val); } // After __syncwarp(), all lanes can read scores_smem[j] float score_j = scores_smem[j]; ``` And for normalization: ```cpp __syncwarp(); for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) { scores_smem[j] = expf(scores_smem[j] - max_score); exp_sum += scores_smem[j]; } // warp reduce exp_sum __syncwarp(); // wait for all lanes to write // All lanes can now read scores_smem[0..N-1] ``` This is much cleaner and avoids register pressure. Let me go with this approach. SMEM layout: - k_smem: N * D_HEAD - v_smem: N * D_HEAD - scores_smem: warps_per_block * N Total = N * (2 * D_HEAD + warps_per_block) For N=32, D_HEAD=16, warps_per_block=32: 32 * (32 + 32) = 2048 floats = 8192 bytes. Fine. Actually wait, v_smem is only needed after softmax. Could I reuse k_smem's space for scores_smem? Not easily because we might still need k during score computation. OK, here's my final kernel code structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # ... CUDA source ... class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0 self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim) self.query = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.key = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.value = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.gate = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.pair_bias = nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads, bias=False) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout) def forward(self, msa_repr, pair_repr, msa_mask): B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape h = self.num_heads d = self.head_dim x = self.layer_norm(msa_repr) q = self.query(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() k = self.key(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() v = self.value(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() bias = self.pair_bias(pair_repr).permute(0, 3, 1, 2).contiguous() mask = msa_mask.to(torch.float32).contiguous() out = torch.empty(B, S, N, D, dtype=msa_repr.dtype, device=msa_repr.device) custom_cuda.msa_attention_forward( q, k, v, g, bias, mask, out, B, S, N, h, d, D ) return self.out_proj(out) ``` Wait, there's another potential issue. My CUDA kernel expects contiguous tensors with specific strides. Let me make sure the `.contiguous()` calls work. After `.view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous()`: - Shape: (B, S, h, N, d) - Strides: contiguous in row-major order Indexing: element [b, s, h_idx, n, d_idx] = ((b * S + s) * h + h_idx) * N * d + n * d + d_idx This matches my kernel's `bsh_offset * N * D_HEAD + n * D_HEAD + d` exactly. Within a warp, thread 0 reads k[0,0], thread 1 reads k[1,0], thread 2 reads k[2,0], etc. These are strided by D_HEAD floats, not contiguous. So they are NOT coalesced! This is the source of the "105x wasted BW" in the profiling. Global memory loads have poor coalescing because consecutive threads access non-consecutive memory locations. With D_HEAD=16, k[j, d] for consecutive j are separated by 16 floats = 64 bytes. So threads 0-31 access addresses 0, 64, 128, 192, ..., 1984. These might span multiple cache lines, causing uncoalesced access. The fix is to restructure so that consecutive threads access consecutive memory. In my new warp-based design: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { dot += q[q_offset + d] * k[qkv_base + j * D_HEAD + d]; } // ... } ``` For a fixed j, lanes 0-15 load k[j, 0..15], which are contiguous in memory. Lanes 16-31 don't participate. This is coalesced (16 out of 32 threads participate, accessing 16 consecutive floats = 64 bytes = one cache line). Then after computing the dot product via shuffle, we move to j+1 and do the same. This pattern is coalesced. So the warp-based approach should have much better memory coalescing. I'll use: - Block size = 1024 threads (32 warps) - Grid = B * S * H * ceil(N / 32) - SMEM for scores per warp - Direct global memory for q, k, v, g Here's the kernel: ```cpp __global__ void msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32; int num_query_blocks = (N + warps_per_block - 1) / warps_per_block; int bsh = blockIdx.x / num_query_blocks; int query_block = blockIdx.x % num_query_blocks; int n = query_block * warps_per_block + warp_id; if (n >= N) return; int b = bsh / (S * H); int rem = bsh % (S * H); int s = rem / H; int h = rem % H; long bsh_offset = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h; long qkv_base = bsh_offset * N * D_HEAD; long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD; // SMEM for scores: each warp gets N floats float* scores = smem + warp_id * N; // Compute scores float max_score = -CUDART_INF_F; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { dot += q[q_offset + d] * k[qkv_base + (long)j * D_HEAD + d]; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { float s_val = dot / sqrtf((float)D_HEAD); s_val += bias[(((long)b * H + h) * N + n) * N + j]; if (mask[((long)b * S + s) * N + j] == 0.0f) { s_val = -CUDART_INF_F; } scores[j] = s_val; max_score = fmaxf(max_score, s_val); } } // Warp-level max for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_score = fmaxf(max_score, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, offset)); } max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0); // Compute exp and sum float exp_sum = 0.0f; if (lane_id < N) { scores[lane_id] = expf(scores[lane_id] - max_score); exp_sum = scores[lane_id]; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { exp_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, offset); } exp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, 0); // Normalize if (lane_id < N) { scores[lane_id] /= exp_sum; } __syncwarp(); // Compute output for each d for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += scores[j] * v[qkv_base + (long)j * D_HEAD + d]; } float gv = g[q_offset + d]; long out_idx = (((long)b * S + s) * N + n) * D + (long)h * D_HEAD + d; out[out_idx] = gv * acc; } } ``` Wait, I changed the exp/sum loop to only use lane_id < N. But with N=32, all 32 lanes participate. This is fine. Actually, there's a potential issue: `if (lane_id < N)` when N=32 means all lanes participate. When N < 32, only lanes 0..N-1 participate. In the score computation: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // ... if (lane_id == 0) { scores[j] = s_val; } } // No __syncwarp() here! ``` Then immediately: ```cpp for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score); } ``` Lane j (where j = lane_id if N <= 32) reads scores[j]. In CUDA, within a warp, if lane 0 writes to SMEM and then lane j reads from the same location, the read might happen before the write unless there's a memory fence or synchronization. However, within a warp, execution is SIMT, and all threads execute the j loop in lockstep. So: For j=0: - All threads compute dot for j=0 - Lane 0 writes scores[0] at the end - The next iteration (j=1) doesn't start until all threads finish j=0 But after the j loop completes, there's no explicit barrier. Actually, for `__shfl_down_sync`, a common pattern for sum is: ```cpp float sum = thread_sum; for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } ``` After this, all lanes have the total sum. Let me think about optimizations for the final output loop: ```cpp for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += scores[j] * v[qkv_base + (long)j * D_HEAD + d]; } float gv = g[q_offset + d]; long out_idx = ... out[out_idx] = gv * acc; } ``` For each d, we read v[j, d] for all j from global memory. Wait, the pattern is: ```cpp for j: for d: read v[j*D_HEAD + d] ``` For a fixed j and d = lane_id, consecutive threads read consecutive memory. Yes, coalesced! But the j loop is sequential. For N=32, we have 32 iterations of j. In each iteration, we read coalesced. This is good. Coalesced. Great! Now let me think about one more optimization: can we vectorize the loads? For D_HEAD=16, if we load float4 (4 floats), we could process 4 elements at once. One more optimization: since `scores` is in shared memory and we load `scores[j]` for all j in the inner loop, this is as fast as register access (or slightly slower). Since `scores` is in shared memory and all threads read the same address `scores[j]`, this is a broadcast. Shared memory broadcasts are very fast (similar to register access). So there's no bank conflict and it's efficient. The `v[...]` access is coalesced as discussed. So the inner loop should run efficiently. I'm now confident in the design. We could compute: ```python combined_mask = bias.unsqueeze(1) # (B, 1, H, N, N) msa_mask_expanded = msa_mask.view(B, S, 1, 1, N).expand(B, S, H, N, N) combined_mask = combined_mask + msa_mask_expanded.float().masked_fill(msa_mask_expanded == 0, float('-inf')) ``` Wait, the bias is added, and the mask sets invalid positions to -inf. BUT we need to be careful: if a position is valid, we add the bias value. If invalid, we want -inf regardless of bias. Actually, the reference does: ```python attn = attn + bias.unsqueeze(1) mask = msa_mask.view(B, S, 1, 1, N).expand_as(attn) attn = attn.masked_fill(mask == 0, float("-inf")) ``` So first add bias, then set masked positions to -inf. If bias at a masked position is large positive, it gets overwritten to -inf. In SDPA, the attn_mask is ADDED to the attention scores before softmax. So if we want to set positions to -inf, we'd add -inf at those positions. So we can create an additive mask: ```python additive_mask = bias.unsqueeze(1).clone() # (B, 1, H, N, N) additive_mask.masked_fill_(msa_mask.view(B, S, 1, 1, N).expand_as(additive_mask) == 0, float('-inf')) ``` Then call SDPA with this additive_mask. But SDPA in PyTorch 2.0 might optimize and use Flash Attention or other backends. For small sizes (N=32), Flash Attention might not be applicable (it typically requires larger sequences for efficiency). So SDPA might fall back to a standard implementation. Also, SDPA adds the attn_mask to Q @ K^T / sqrt(d). Then applies softmax. Then computes attention @ V. This matches our reference. But SDPA doesn't support gating (the `g * out` part). We'd still need to apply that after SDPA. Let me just use the custom CUDA kernel. It's more fun and potentially faster for this specific pattern. OK, writing the final code now. For real this time! Actually, I just want to double-check one thing about the warp shuffle functions. In CUDA, `__shfl_down_sync` is: ```cpp T __shfl_down_sync(unsigned mask, T var, unsigned int delta, int width=warpSize); ``` It returns the value of `var` from the thread with ID `threadIdx.x + delta` within the warp (or subgroup of size `width`). The `mask` specifies which threads participate. For delta=16: thread 0 gets thread 16's value, thread 1 gets thread 17's, etc. For delta=8: thread 0 gets thread 8's, etc. In my reduction: ```cpp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset); } ``` This is a standard sum reduction. After offset=16, lanes 0-15 have sum of lanes 0-15 and 16-31. After offset=8, lanes 0-7 have sum of 0-7 and 8-15. After offset=4, lanes 0-3 have sum of 0-3 and 4-7. After offset=2, lanes 0-1 have sum of 0-1 and 2-3. After offset=1, lane 0 has total sum. All lanes end up with some partial sum. If we only need the total in lane 0, we can broadcast at the end. If we need it in all lanes, we can do: ```cpp dot = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, dot, 0); ``` Wait, but after the reduction loop, lane 0 has the total. Other lanes have partial sums. If we do: ```cpp dot = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, dot, 0); ``` All lanes now have lane 0's total. This is more efficient than doing the full butterfly reduction across all lanes. Let me use this pattern in my code: ```cpp // Sum reduction: only need total in lane 0 for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset); } dot = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, dot, 0); ``` Wait, the first loop computes the total in lane 0 (and some partials in others). Then `__shfl_sync(0xffffffff, dot, 0)` broadcasts lane 0's total to all lanes. This gives all lanes the total sum. It's equivalent to a full reduction but more efficient. But I only need the total in lane 0 for score computation (the `if (lane_id == 0)` block). And for the exp_sum, I need all lanes to have the total. Let me structure accordingly: For dot product: ```cpp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { // use dot which is the total sum in lane 0 } ``` Since only lane 0 needs dot, I skip the broadcast. For exp_sum, I need all lanes to have it: ```cpp float exp_sum = 0.0f; // compute partial sums for (...) { exp_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, offset); } exp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, 0); ``` Wait, actually, after the shuffle down reduction, lane 0 has the total. Then `__shfl_sync(..., exp_sum, 0)` broadcasts lane 0's value to all lanes. This is correct and efficient. But I can also just use the full shuffle down without the final broadcast, and then only lane 0 has the correct value. Let me be careful about what each lane needs. For max_score: - I compute it in lane 0 during the score loop - Then reduce across the warp - Then broadcast to all lanes (for the exp computation) Actually, for the `expf(scores[j] - max_score)` computation, all lanes that read scores[j] need max_score. But since the broadcast happens before the exp loop, all lanes get max_score. Good. OK, I'll write the code with proper shuffle patterns. Wait, I also realize that for `__shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, offset)`, if max_score is -inf for non-lane-0 threads, the reduction works correctly: fmaxf(-inf, other_value) returns the other value when propagated correctly. Let me trace through for max_score: Initial: - lane 0: max_score = some_value - lanes 1-31: max_score = -inf offset=16: - lane 0: gets lane 16's -inf, fmaxf(some_value, -inf) = some_value - lane 1: gets lane 17's -inf, fmaxf(-inf, -inf) = -inf - ... - lane 15: gets lane 31's -inf, fmaxf(-inf, -inf) = -inf offset=8: - lane 0: gets lane 8's -inf, stays some_value - lane 1: gets lane 9's -inf, stays -inf - ... offset=1: - lane 0: gets lane 1's -inf, stays some_value After loop: lane 0 has some_value, lanes 1-31 have -inf. Then `max_score = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_score, 0);` All lanes get some_value. Correct! Great. For exp_sum: Similar logic, but all lanes start with valid partial sums. OK, I'm confident the CUDA code is correct. Let me write it. Actually, there's one more subtle thing about the `scores` array in SMEM. In the exp loop: ```cpp for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score); exp_sum += scores[j]; } ``` For j where `j >= N`, the loop doesn't execute. So `scores[j]` is not updated. That's fine because those values are never read later. For j where `j < N`, `scores[j]` was written by lane 0 in the score loop. After `__syncwarp()` between the loops, the write is visible. Then in the normalize loop: ```cpp for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) { scores[j] /= exp_sum; } ``` Each lane updates its own scores[j]. After `__syncwarp()`, all normalized scores are visible. Then in the output loop: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { acc += scores[j] * v[...]; } ``` All lanes read all scores[0..N-1]. Since we just did `__syncwarp()`, this is safe. Wait, but what about `__syncwarp()` placement? Let me trace through: 1. Score loop: lane 0 writes scores[j] for j=0..N-1 2. `__syncwarp()` - now all lanes see scores[0..N-1] 3. Max reduction and broadcast - all lanes have max_score 4. Exp loop: each lane updates its scores[j] and accumulates exp_sum 5. Exp sum reduction and broadcast - all lanes have exp_sum 6. Normalize loop: each lane updates its scores[j] 7. `__syncwarp()` - now all lanes see normalized scores 8. Output loop: all lanes read all scores[j] This is correct. We need `__syncwarp()` at steps 2 and 7. But wait, between steps 4 and 5, do we need a sync? In step 4, each lane reads `scores[j]` (written by lane 0 in step 1) and writes a new value. The reads happen before the writes for the same j. But different j values are written by different lanes. For example, lane 0 writes scores[0], lane 1 writes scores[1], etc. There's no conflict because each lane writes to a different address. However, within a warp, memory operations might be reordered. To ensure the writes are visible before moving to the reduction, we might need a sync. Actually, in the SIMT execution model, all threads in a warp execute instructions in lockstep. So for a given j iteration in the exp loop: - All lanes read scores[j] for their assigned j - All lanes compute exp - All lanes write back to scores[j] But wait, different lanes have different j in the same instruction cycle! Actually, no. In the loop `for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32)`, each lane executes a different number of iterations and has different j values in each "cycle" of the warp. Actually, because of divergence in the loop iterations, the execution is not perfectly lockstep across all iterations. But for a warp, the hardware executes all active threads together. Since all threads are active (none are predicated off in this loop condition except when j >= N), and the writes to different SMEM addresses should not conflict... To be absolutely safe, I should add a `__syncwarp()` after the normalize loop (which I already have) and after the score loop (which I also already have). Between the exp loop and the normalize loop, we do the reduction, which uses shuffle and doesn't access SMEM. So no sync needed there. Actually, let me think again. After the score loop, I need to sync before reading scores in the exp loop. I have that sync. After the normalize loop, I need to sync before reading scores in the output loop. I have that sync too. Yes, the exp_sum reduction uses shuffle, not SMEM. So no SMEM reads between the exp loop write and the normalize loop read. But the normalize loop reads scores[j] for the same j that the exp loop just wrote. Within a single thread, the write and subsequent read happen in order. Across threads, lane k reads scores[k] which was just written by lane k. No conflict. So no sync is needed between the exp loop and normalize loop. Actually wait, what if a lane wants to read scores[j] that was written by another lane? In the normalize loop, each lane only reads and writes its own scores[j] (where j = lane_id + k*32). Here's my complete Python source: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math_constants.h> __global__ void __launch_bounds__(1024) msa_attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ g, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mask, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; } """ CPP_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> void msa_attention_forward( torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v, torch::Tensor g, torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor mask, torch::Tensor out, int B, int S, int N, int H, int D_HEAD, int D); """ custom_cuda = load_inline( name="msa_attention", cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCE, cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["msa_attention_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0 self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim) self.query = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.key = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.value = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False) self.gate = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.pair_bias = nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads, bias=False) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout) def forward( self, msa_repr: torch.Tensor, pair_repr: torch.Tensor, msa_mask: torch.Tensor, ) -> torch.Tensor: B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape h = self.num_heads d = self.head_dim x = self.layer_norm(msa_repr) q = self.query(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() k = self.key(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() v = self.value(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous() bias = self.pair_bias(pair_repr).permute(0, 3, 1, 2).contiguous() mask = msa_mask.to(torch.float32).contiguous() out = torch.empty(B, S, N, D, dtype=msa_repr.dtype, device=msa_repr.device) custom_cuda.msa_attention_forward( q, k, v, g, bias, mask, out, B, S, N, h, d, D ) return self.out_proj(out) ``` Wait, I want to double check the SMEM size computation. I'll add it. Actually, I just realized: in the kernel, I use `extern __shared__ float smem[];` and allocate `warps_per_block * N` floats. If N is a runtime value, the compiler doesn't know the exact layout. But since we access `smem + warp_id * N`, and warp_id ranges 0 to warps_per_block-1, this is fine. The compiler just needs to know the total size, which we pass in the kernel launch. One more thing: `__launch_bounds__(1024)` tells the compiler the max threads per block is 1024. But if we launch with fewer threads, the compiler still optimizes for 1024. This is fine because we don't exceed 1024. But if we reduce threads to 512, the compiler assumed 1024 and might use more registers than needed. This could reduce occupancy for the 512-thread launch. To be safe, I should either: 1. Remove `__launch_bounds__` entirely 2. Or use a smaller bound if we might launch with fewer threads Without `__launch_bounds__`, the compiler uses a default heuristic. For small kernels, this should be fine. I'll remove `__launch_bounds__` to avoid issues with dynamic block sizes. Actually, for dynamic block sizes, there isn't a straightforward way to set `__launch_bounds__` correctly without template specialization. I'll just remove it. Alright, FINAL code! Let me also add error checking to the host function for CUDA errors: ```cpp void msa_attention_forward(...) { // ... launch ... cudaError_t err = cudaGetLastError(); if (err != cudaSuccess) { printf("CUDA error: %s\n", cudaGetErrorString(err)); } } ``` This is helpful for debugging. I'll add it. Actually, I don't want to print in production, but for debugging failed compiles/runs it's useful. I'll keep it. OK, now I'll produce the complete Python code.

bsh_offset * N * D_HEAD;\n long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD;\n \n float* scores = smem + warp_id * N;\n const float scale = rsqrtf((float)D_HEAD);\n \n float max_score = -CUDART_INF_F;\n for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {\n float dot = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) {\n dot += q[q_offset + d] * k[qkv_base + (long)j * D_HEAD + d];\n }\n #pragma unroll\n * N + j];\n if (mask[((long)b * S + s) * N + j] == 0.0f) {\n s_val = -CUDART_INF_F;\n }\n scores[j] = s_val;\n max_score = fmaxf(max_score, s_val);\n }\n }\n __syncwarp();\n \n #pragma unroll\n for exp_sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) {\n scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score);\n exp_sum += scores[j];\n }\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n exp_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, offset);\n }\n exp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, exp_sum, 0);\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = lane_id; j < N; j += 32) {\n scores[j] /= exp_sum;\n }\n __syncwarp();\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = lane_id; d < D_HEAD; d += 32) {\n float acc = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {\n acc += scores[j] * v[qkv_base + (long)j * D_HEAD + d];\n }\n float gv = g[q_offset + d];\n long out_idx = (((long)b * S + s) * N + n) * D + (long)h * D_HEAD + d;\n out[out_idx] = gv * acc;\n }\n}\n\nvoid cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCE,\n cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE,\n functions=[\"msa_attention_forward\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0):\n super().__init__()\n self.num_heads = num_heads\n self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads\n assert msa_dim % num_heads == 0\n\n self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)\n self.query = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)\n self.key = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)\n self.value = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)\n self.gate = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)\n self.pair_bias = nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads, bias=False)\n self.out_proj = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)\n self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)\n\n def forward(\n self,\n msa_repr: torch.Tensor,\n pair_repr: torch.Tensor,\n msa_mask: torch.Tensor,\n ) -> torch.Tensor:\n B, S, N, D = msa_repr.shape\n h = self.num_heads\n d = self.head_dim\n\n x = self.layer_norm(msa_repr)\n q = self.query(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous()\n k = self.key(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous()\n v = self.value(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous()\n g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).contiguous()\n bias = self.pair_bias(pair_repr).permute(0, 3, 1, 2).contiguous()\n \n mask = msa_mask.to(torch.float32).contiguous()\n out = torch.empty(B, S, N, D, dtype=msa_repr.dtype, device=msa_repr.device)\n\n H, int D_HEAD, int D\n) {\n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n \n float* k_smem = smem;\n float* v_smem = k_smem + N * D_HEAD;\n float* scores = v_smem + N * D_HEAD;\n \n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int warp_id = tid / 32;\n int lane_id = tid % 32;\n int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32;\n int num_query_blocks = (N + warps_per_block - 1) / warps_per_block;\n \n int bsh = blockIdx.x / num_query_blocks;\n int query_block = blockIdx.x % num_query_blocks;\n int n = query_block * warps_per_block + warp_id;\n \n int b = bsh / (S * H);\n int rem = bsh % (S * H);\n int s = rem / H;\n int h = rem % H;\n \n long bsh_offset = ((long)b * S + s) * H + h;\n long qkv_base = bsh_offset * N * D_HEAD;\n \n // Load K and V into shared memory\n if (tid < N * D_HEAD) {\n k_smem[tid] = k[qkv_base + tid];\n v_smem[tid] = v[qkv_base + tid];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n if (n >= N) return;\n \n long q_offset = qkv_base + (long)n * D_HEAD;\n float* my_scores = scores + warp_id * N;\n const float scale = rsqrtf((float)D_HEAD);\n \n float q_local = 0.0f;\n if (lane_id < D_HEAD) {\n q_local = q[q_offset + lane_id];\n }\n \n float max_score = -CUDART_INF_F;\n for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {\n float dot = 0.0f;\n if (lane_id < D_HEAD) {\n dot += q_local * k_smem[j * D_HEAD + lane_id];\n }\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot, offset);\n }\n if (lane_id == 0) {\n float s_val = dot * scale;\n s_val += bias[(((long)b * H + h) * N + n) * N + j];\n if (mask[((long)b * S + s) * N + j] == 0.0f) {\n s_val = -CUDART_INF_F;\n turn 8llm latency: 14.1s
reasoning
The compilation passed. This kernel tries to load all K and V for the entire (b,s,h) into shared memory, reducing global memory access for K and V lookups. Let me check correctness and profile. The shared memory required is: - scores: warps_per_block * N - K: N * D_HEAD - V: N * D_HEAD With N=32, D_HEAD=16: - K+V = 32*16*2 = 1024 floats - scores = warps_per_block * 32 With warps_per_block=16: - scores = 512 floats - Total = 1536 floats = 6KB, well under 48KB. Good. Then threads=16*32=512. Let me check correctness.

turn 10llm latency: 81.0s
reasoning
The global load coalescing is still very bad. The issue is that each lane in a warp is accessing different elements of K and V when computing the dot product. Let me rethink the approach. So thread 0 reads q[0..15] and k[0..15], thread 1 reads q[0..15] and k[16..31], etc. So in a warp of 32 threads, at iteration d: - Thread 0 reads q[q_offset+d] and k[qkv_base+0*D_HEAD+d] - Thread 1 reads q[q_offset+d] and k[qkv_base+1*D_HEAD+d] - ... - Thread 31 reads q[q_offset+d] and k[qkv_base+31*D_HEAD+d] For Q access: all threads in the warp read the SAME address q[q_offset+d]. This broadcasts from L1/L2 cache, which is efficient! For K access: thread i reads k[qkv_base+i*D_HEAD+d]. Adjacent threads read elements D_HEAD apart. With D_HEAD=16, that's 64 bytes. A cache line is 128 bytes, so every other thread reads from the same cache line. This is NOT coalesced well - 2 threads share a cache line, so we need 16 cache lines instead of 1. Actually wait, global memory loads work in terms of memory sectors (32 bytes). For 32 threads, a fully coalesced access would use 1 sector (if contiguous and aligned), or 2 sectors if 64 bytes, etc. With threads reading elements 64 bytes apart: - Thread 0: address A - Thread 1: address A+64 - Thread 2: address A+128 - ... - Thread 31: address A+31*64 = A+1984 These span almost 2KB! The memory coalescing unit would need to issue many separate load instructions because threads are requesting memory from many different sectors. This is the coalescing problem. If I restructure so that adjacent threads read adjacent memory, I can fix this. Hmm, this doesn't help much. Actually, a key insight: PyTorch's Linear layer operates on the last dimension. For input (B, S, N, D), it applies D_out = D_in @ W^T. If we want output (B, S, N, H, D_HEAD), the natural way is to have W of shape (D, H*D_HEAD) and then reshape. The permute(0,1,3,2,4) just swaps the N and H dimensions. Since each (b,h,n) processes all j sequentially, the bias row for n will be loaded into cache. The next j+1 is at address+4 bytes, which is in the same cache line. So after the first load (128 bytes cache line), the next 31 loads are L1 hits. Total: 1 DRAM transaction + 31 L1 hits. That's very efficient! So bias loading isn't the problem. Let me focus on trying a configuration with more parallelism. What if I process multiple heads per block? For B=2, S=8, H=4, N=32, D_HEAD=16: Total work = 2*8*4*32 = 2048 queries. With 16 warps/block processing 16 queries each (1 per warp), I need 2048/16=128 blocks. But H100 has 132 SMs, so 128 blocks is barely enough to fill all SMs. What if I use 1 warp per block (32 threads)? Then I need 2048 blocks. That's more blocks than SMs, which is good for occupancy. But each block would have very few threads. Let me try 1 warp per block: - Block = 32 threads - Each block handles one (b, s, h, n) - Grid = 2048 blocks - Shared mem per block = 2*N*D_HEAD + N = 2*32*16 + 32 = 1056 floats = 4224 bytes - Very low shared mem, very low register demand per block This should allow many blocks per SM and better occupancy. Let me try this approach. Actually, with 1 warp per block, the K/V loading to shared memory: ```c if (tid < N * D_HEAD) { k_smem[tid] = k[qkv_base + tid]; v_smem[tid] = v[qkv_base + tid]; } ``` 32 threads loading 1024 elements = 32 loads per thread, sequential. Good coalescing within each warp. But multiple blocks from different (b,s,h) loading the same K/V will create redundant loads. Not a big issue at this scale. And the Q, G, and out accesses are also good within a warp. Let me try the 1-warp-per-block approach.
call → compile_kernel

< N; ++j) {\n my_scores[j] = expf(my_scores[j] - max_score);\n exp_sum += my_scores[j];\n }\n \n #pragma unroll\n for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) {\n my_scores[j] /= exp_sum;\n }\n \n // Weighted sum of
